on $\bar{B}^{(g)}(\mathcal{A})$ by $k$ base degrees
(see Remark~\ref{rem:differential-notation} below).

The strict nilpotence condition $\Dg{g}^{\,2} = 0$ decomposes into:
\begin{align}
(d^{(0)})^2 &= 0 \quad \text{(genus 0 exactness)}\\
\{d^{(0)}, d^{(1)}\} &= 0 \quad \text{(genus 1 compatibility)}\\
\{d^{(0)}, d^{(2)}\} + (d^{(1)})^2 &= 0 \quad \text{(genus 2 relation)}\\
&\vdots
\end{align}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof via spectral sequence]
Consider the Leray spectral sequence for the universal configuration space fibration:
\begin{equation}
\pi: \overline{\mathcal{C}}_{g,n} \to \overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g}
\end{equation}
where $\overline{\mathcal{C}}_{g,n}$ is the relative Fulton--MacPherson compactification of $n$ points on the universal curve over~$\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$. The fiber over $[\Sigma_g] \in \overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$ is $\overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g)$.

\emph{Step 1: Fiberwise differential.} On each fiber $\overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g)$, the differential $d^{(0)}$ is the genus-zero bar differential using residues at collision divisors. By Theorem~\ref{thm:bar-nilpotency-complete}, $(d^{(0)})^2 = 0$.

\emph{Step 2: Base contributions.} The differential $d^{(1)}$ arises from the Gauss--Manin connection on the local system $R^q\pi_*(\mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes n} \otimes \Omega^*_{\log})$ over $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$. The compatibility $\{d^{(0)}, d^{(1)}\} = 0$ follows from flatness of the Gauss--Manin connection (Stokes' theorem applied fiberwise).

\emph{Step 3: Higher corrections.} Terms $d^{(k)}$ for $k \geq 2$ arise from the Leray filtration: $d^{(k)}$ maps $F^p \to F^{p+k}$ in the filtration by base degree. The relations ensuring $\Dg{g}^{\,2} = 0$ (e.g., $\{d^{(0)}, d^{(2)}\} + (d^{(1)})^2 = 0$) follow from the associativity of the Leray filtration and the exactness of the total complex.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Leray decomposition and Convention~\ref{conv:higher-genus-differentials}]
\label{rem:differential-notation}
\index{bar differential!notation}
The superscript~$(k)$ in $\Dg{g}=d^{(0)}+d^{(1)}+\cdots$ denotes
Leray filtration degree, not genus: $d^{(0)}=\dzero$ (fiberwise
collisions), $d^{(1)}$ (Gauss--Manin), $d^{(k\geq 2)}$ (higher
corrections).  The total $\Dg{g}^{\,2}=0$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:quantum-diff-squares-zero}), while the fiberwise
$\dfib$ is curved: $\dfib^{\,2}=\kappa(\cA)\cdot\omega_g$.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Conceptual decomposition of the modular bar differential]
\label{rem:modular-differential-decomposition}
\index{bar differential!modular decomposition}
The modular bar differential has three geometric components:
(i)~$d_{\mathrm{int}}$ (internal, within $d^{(0)}$);
(ii)~$d_{\mathrm{sep}}$ (separating edge contraction, splitting
$g=g_1+g_2$);
(iii)~$d_{\mathrm{ns}}$ (non-separating edge contraction,
genus-raising).
Nilpotence $d^2=0$ follows from $\partial^2=0$ on the boundary
stratification of $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Explicit form of quantum corrections}

\begin{theorem}[Concrete quantum differential; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:concrete-quantum-differential}
For $\alpha \in \bar{B}^{(g)}_n(\mathcal{A})$ represented by:
\begin{equation}
\alpha = \int_{\overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g)} \phi_1(p_1) \cdots \phi_n(p_n) \cdot f(p_1, \ldots, p_n; \Omega) \cdot \prod_{i<j} \eta_{ij}^{(g)}
\end{equation}

The total corrected differential decomposes by Leray degree
\textup{(}Remark~\textup{\ref{rem:differential-notation})}:
\[
\Dg{g} = d^{(0)} + d^{(1)} + \cdots
\]
and acts by:
\begin{align}
d^{(0)}\alpha &= \sum_{i<j} \mathrm{Res}_{D_{ij}} [\mu_{ij}(\phi_i \otimes \phi_j) \otimes \mathrm{remaining}]\\
d^{(1)}\alpha &= \sum_{\gamma \in H_1(\Sigma_g)} \oint_\gamma \omega_\gamma \cdot \delta_{\gamma^*}[\alpha]\\
d^{(k)}\alpha &= \sum_{\mathrm{strata}\; \Delta} \int_\Delta \mathrm{(boundary\; contribution)}, \qquad k \ge 2
\end{align}
where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $\mu_{ij}$ is the chiral product of $\phi_i, \phi_j$
\item $\omega_\gamma$ are 1-forms dual to cycles $\gamma$
\item $\delta_{\gamma^*}$ inserts a puncture along the dual cycle
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The decomposition follows from the stratification of $\overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g)$ by boundary type.

\emph{Component $d^{(0)}$: Collision residues (genus-preserving).}
The collision divisors $D_{ij} = \{p_i = p_j\} \subset \overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g)$ are present at every genus. The residue at $D_{ij}$ extracts the singular part of the OPE $\phi_i(p_i) \phi_j(p_j)$, which is the chiral product $\mu_{ij}(\phi_i \otimes \phi_j)$. This component preserves the genus of the underlying curve and reduces the number of points by one (replacing $p_i, p_j$ by a single merged point). The formula is:
\[
d^{(0)}\alpha = \sum_{i<j} \mathrm{Res}_{p_i = p_j}\!\left[\phi_i(p_i) \phi_j(p_j) \cdot \eta_{ij}^{(g)}\right] \otimes \left(\bigotimes_{k \neq i,j} \phi_k(p_k)\right) \otimes \eta_{\mathrm{remaining}}
\]
This is the direct genus-$g$ analog of the genus-0 bar differential.

\emph{Component $d^{(1)}$: Cycle insertions (Leray degree $1$).}
On $\Sigma_g$, the non-trivial 1-cycles $\gamma \in H_1(\Sigma_g, \mathbb{Z})$ contribute to the differential via the quantum-corrected Arnold relation. At genus~$g$, the logarithmic form $\eta_{ij}^{(g)}$ is not closed: $d_{\mathrm{dR}}\eta_{ij}^{(g)}$ picks up contributions from the periods of $\Sigma_g$ (cf.\ Example~\ref{ex:heisenberg-genus-1}, where $d_{\mathrm{dR}}\eta_{12}^{(1)} = 2\pi i\, \omega_\tau$ at genus~1). The operator $\delta_{\gamma^*}$ inserts a ``virtual puncture'' along the dual cycle $\gamma^*$, which geometrically corresponds to a degeneration of the curve~$\Sigma_g$ in which a separating cycle collapses.

\emph{Component $d^{(k)}$ ($k \ge 2$): Boundary-strata contributions.}
Higher-genus corrections arise from the boundary strata $\Delta$ of the Deligne--Mumford compactification $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$. Each boundary stratum $\Delta$ corresponds to either:
\begin{itemize}
\item \emph{Separating degeneration}: $\Sigma_g \rightsquigarrow \Sigma_{g_1} \cup \Sigma_{g_2}$ with $g_1 + g_2 = g$, contributing a term involving the tensor product of lower-genus bar complexes.
\item \emph{Non-separating degeneration}: $\Sigma_g \rightsquigarrow \Sigma_{g-1}$ with a self-sewing node, contributing a term with a trace over the node.
\end{itemize}
The integral $\int_\Delta$ extracts the residue at the boundary divisor, and the sum is over all strata~$\Delta$ of $\partial\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$.

\emph{Exhaustiveness.} The components $d^{(0)}, d^{(1)}, \ldots$ exhaust the full differential because the boundary of $\overline{C}_n(\Sigma_g) \subset \overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$ is the union of collision divisors (giving $d^{(0)}$) and moduli boundary strata (contributing to $d^{(k)}$ for $k \ge 1$). The identity $\Dg{g}^{\,2} = 0$ decomposes into relations
\[
\sum_{a+b=r} d^{(a)} \circ d^{(b)} = 0
\]
for each Leray degree $r$, encoding consistency across the correction tower.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Explicit genus 1 example: central extensions}

\begin{example}[Heisenberg central extension from genus 1]
\label{ex:heisenberg-genus-1}
\index{central extension!higher genus}
For the Heisenberg vertex algebra $\mathcal{H}$ with current $J(z) = \sum_{n \in \mathbb{Z}} a_n z^{-n-1}$:

\emph{Genus 0.} At genus $0$, the bar differential gives:
\begin{equation}
d^{(0)}[J \otimes J] = [J, J]_{g=0} = 0
\end{equation}
There is no central extension at genus zero.

\emph{Genus 1.} Consider the trace element:
\begin{equation}
\text{Tr}^{(1)}[J \otimes J] = \oint_{S^1} J(z) \otimes J(z) \; dz
\end{equation}
where the integral is over the meridian circle of the torus.

Computing the Leray-degree-$1$ contribution:
\begin{align}
d^{(1)}[\text{Tr}^{(1)}(J \otimes J)] &= \int_{E_\tau} d_{\mathrm{dR}}\!\left(J(z_1) \otimes J(z_2) \cdot \eta_{12}^{(1)}\right)\\
&= \int_{E_\tau} \left[\partial_{z_1} J(z_1) \cdot J(z_2) + J(z_1) \cdot \partial_{z_2} J(z_2)\right] \eta_{12}^{(1)}\\
&\quad + \int_{E_\tau} J(z_1) \otimes J(z_2) \cdot d_{\mathrm{dR}}\eta_{12}^{(1)}
\end{align}

Using the quantum-corrected Arnold relation
$d_{\mathrm{dR}}\eta_{12}^{(1)} = 2\pi i \omega_\tau$:
\begin{equation}
d^{(1)}[\text{Tr}^{(1)}(J \otimes J)] = \kappa \cdot [1]^{(1)}
\end{equation}
where $\kappa$ is the central charge and $[1]^{(1)}$ is the genus-1 identity element.

This is the \emph{central extension} $[J, J] = \kappa \cdot c$ emerging from genus-1 quantum geometry.
\end{example}

% ================================================================
% NEW COMPREHENSIVE SECTION: GENUS 1-3 COMPLETE TREATMENT
% ================================================================

\section{\texorpdfstring{Genus $1$: the elliptic bar complex}{Genus 1: the elliptic bar complex}}
\label{sec:genus-1-elliptic-complete}

\subsection{Motivation: where quantum corrections begin}

At genus~$0$, the bar differential satisfies $\dzero^2 = 0$ exactly,
the propagator is the rational function $(z - w)^{-1}$, and there
are no moduli.  At genus~$1$, all three change: the propagator
becomes the elliptic function $\theta_1'(z-w|\tau)/\theta_1(z-w|\tau)$,
the modular parameter $\tau$ parametrizes a one-dimensional moduli
space, and the fiberwise differential acquires curvature $\dfib^{\,2} = \kappa(\cA)\cdot\omega_1$ proportional to the central
charge.  This single step, from genus~$0$ to genus~$1$, is
the birth of the modular theory.

In the language of Remark~\ref{rem:nilpotence-periodicity}: at
genus~$0$ the logarithm $\eta = d\log(z-w)$ is single-valued and
nilpotence is exact; at genus~$1$ the logarithm
$d\log\theta_1(z-w|\tau)$ acquires monodromy~$2\pi i$ around the
$B$-cycle (since
$\theta_1(z + \tau|\tau) = -e^{-\pi i\tau - 2\pi i z}\,
\theta_1(z|\tau)$), and this monodromy is the \emph{exponential} of
the nilpotence defect~$\kappa\cdot\omega_1$.  The birth of the
modular theory is the birth of non-trivial monodromy of the
categorical logarithm.

\subsection{Elliptic curves and modular parameter}

\begin{definition}[Elliptic curve \texorpdfstring{$E_\tau$}{E-tau}]
\label{def:elliptic-curve-tau}
Fix $\tau \in \mathbb{H} = \{z \in \mathbb{C} : \text{Im}(z) > 0\}$ (upper half-plane).
The elliptic curve is:
\begin{equation}
E_\tau = \mathbb{C} / \Lambda_\tau, \quad \Lambda_\tau = \mathbb{Z} \oplus \tau\mathbb{Z}
\end{equation}

The complex structure is classified by the $j$-invariant $j(\tau) = 1728\,(E_4(\tau))^3/((E_4(\tau))^3 - (E_6(\tau))^2)$, the modular group $SL_2(\mathbb{Z})$ acts by $\tau \mapsto (a\tau + b)/(c\tau + d)$, and $\text{Vol}(E_\tau) = \text{Im}(\tau)$.
\end{definition}

\subsection{Weierstrass functions}

\begin{definition}[Weierstrass \texorpdfstring{$\wp$}{p}-function]
\label{def:weierstrass-p}
The fundamental elliptic function is:
\begin{equation}
\wp(z|\tau) = \frac{1}{z^2} + \sum_{\omega \in \Lambda_\tau \setminus \{0\}} 
\left(\frac{1}{(z-\omega)^2} - \frac{1}{\omega^2}\right)
\end{equation}

It is elliptic ($\wp(z + \omega) = \wp(z)$), has a double pole at $z = 0$, expansion $\wp(z) = z^{-2} + 3G_4(\tau) z^2 + 5G_6(\tau) z^4 + O(z^6)$, and satisfies $\wp'(z)^2 = 4\wp(z)^3 - g_2\wp(z) - g_3$ with $g_2 = 60G_4$, $g_3 = 140G_6$.
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Connection to configuration spaces]
\label{rem:wp-config-space}
The chiral propagator on $E_\tau$ is
$K(z,w|\tau) = \frac{\theta_1'(z-w|\tau)}{\theta_1(z-w|\tau)} \, dz = \left(\zeta(z-w) - (z-w)G_2(\tau)\right) dz$.
Its quasi-periodicity $K(z+\tau) = K(z) - 2\pi i\, dz$ is the source of genus-$1$ quantum corrections.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Eisenstein series and quasi-modular forms}

\begin{definition}[Eisenstein series \texorpdfstring{$E_{2k}$}{E(2k)}]
\label{def:eisenstein}
For $k \geq 2$:
\begin{equation}
E_{2k}(\tau) = 1 - \frac{4k}{B_{2k}} \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma_{2k-1}(n) q^n, \quad q = e^{2\pi i \tau}
\end{equation}
where $\sigma_r(n) = \sum_{d|n} d^r$ and $B_{2k}$ are Bernoulli numbers.

\emph{First few values}:
\begin{align}
E_2(\tau) &= 1 - 24\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma_1(n) q^n = 1 - 24q - 72q^2 - 96q^3 - \cdots \\
E_4(\tau) &= 1 + 240\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma_3(n) q^n = 1 + 240q + 2160q^2 + \cdots \\
E_6(\tau) &= 1 - 504\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \sigma_5(n) q^n = 1 - 504q - 16632q^2 - \cdots
\end{align}
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Modular vs quasi-modular {\cite{KP84}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:modular-vs-quasi}
Under $\gamma = \begin{pmatrix} a & b \\ c & d \end{pmatrix} \in SL_2(\mathbb{Z})$ with $\tau' = \frac{a\tau+b}{c\tau+d}$:

\emph{Modular forms} ($k \geq 4$, even):
\begin{equation}
E_{2k}\left(\frac{a\tau+b}{c\tau+d}\right) = (c\tau + d)^{2k} E_{2k}(\tau)
\end{equation}

\emph{Quasi-modular} ($k=2$):
\begin{equation}
E_2\left(\frac{a\tau+b}{c\tau+d}\right) = (c\tau + d)^2 E_2(\tau) + \frac{6c(c\tau+d)}{\pi i}
\end{equation}

The anomaly term $\frac{6c(c\tau+d)}{\pi i}$ (with positive sign, verified by direct computation for $\tau \mapsto -1/\tau$: setting $a=0,b=-1,c=1,d=0$ gives $E_2(-1/\tau) = \tau^2 E_2(\tau) + 6\tau/(\pi i)$) is the modular anomaly, the source of quantum corrections at genus one.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Origin of the anomaly]
The Eisenstein series $E_2$ arises from the non-convergent sum:
\begin{equation}
E_2(\tau) = 1 - 24\sum_{\omega \in \Lambda_\tau \setminus \{0\}} \frac{1}{\omega^2}
\end{equation}

This sum requires regularization. The standard method (Eisenstein summation) introduces a cutoff that breaks modular invariance, leaving the anomaly term.

\emph{Connection to central charge}: For a chiral algebra with central charge $c$, the genus-1
partition function $Z_1(\tau)$ satisfies:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\partial}{\partial \bar{\tau}} \log Z_1(\tau) = -\frac{c}{24\pi \text{Im}(\tau)}
\end{equation}

This holomorphic anomaly is measured precisely by $E_2(\tau)$.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Theta functions}

\begin{definition}[Jacobi theta functions]
\label{def:jacobi-theta}
The four theta functions with characteristics $[\alpha, \beta]$ where $\alpha, \beta \in \{0, 1/2\}$:

\begin{align}
\vartheta[\alpha, \beta](z|\tau) &= \sum_{n \in \mathbb{Z}} \exp\left(\pi i (n+\alpha)^2 \tau + 2\pi i (n+\alpha)(z+\beta)\right)
\end{align}

\emph{Standard notation}:
\begin{align}
\vartheta_1(z|\tau) &= \vartheta[1/2, 1/2](z|\tau) \quad \text{(odd, vanishes at } z=0\text{)} \\
\vartheta_2(z|\tau) &= \vartheta[1/2, 0](z|\tau) \quad \text{(even)} \\
\vartheta_3(z|\tau) &= \vartheta[0, 0](z|\tau) \quad \text{(even)} \\
\vartheta_4(z|\tau) &= \vartheta[0, 1/2](z|\tau) \quad \text{(even)}
\end{align}

\emph{Product formula for $\vartheta_1$}:
\begin{equation}
\vartheta_1(z|\tau) = 2q^{1/8}\sin(\pi z) \prod_{n=1}^{\infty} (1-q^n)(1-q^n e^{2\pi i z})(1-q^n e^{-2\pi i z})
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Theta zero values {\cite{Fay73}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:theta-zero}
At $z = 0$:
\begin{align}
\vartheta_1(0|\tau) &= 0 \quad \text{(vanishes)} \\
\vartheta_2(0|\tau) &= 2q^{1/8} \prod_{n=1}^{\infty} (1-q^n)(1+q^n)^2 \\
\vartheta_3(0|\tau) &= \prod_{n=1}^{\infty} (1-q^n)(1+q^{n-1/2})^2 \\
\vartheta_4(0|\tau) &= \prod_{n=1}^{\infty} (1-q^n)(1-q^{n-1/2})^2
\end{align}

These are \emph{modular forms of weight $\frac{1}{2}$} (for $\Gamma_0(4)$ with appropriate multiplier systems).
\end{theorem}

\subsection{The genus-1 bar differential: explicit construction}

\begin{definition}[Elliptic logarithmic form]
\label{def:elliptic-log-form}
On $E_\tau$, the logarithmic 1-form between points $z_i, z_j \in E_\tau$ is:
\begin{equation}
\eta_{ij}^{(1)} = \frac{\vartheta_1'(z_i - z_j \mid \tau)}{\vartheta_1(z_i - z_j \mid \tau)}(dz_i - dz_j)
\end{equation}

The theta part $\vartheta_1'/\vartheta_1$ is the elliptic analog of $1/(z_i - z_j)$, while the quasi-periodicity shift $z_i \to z_i + \tau$ produces $-2\pi i\,(dz_i - dz_j)$; this shift is the genus-1 obstruction.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Properties of \texorpdfstring{$\eta_{ij}^{(1)}$}{eta-ij(1)}; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:eta-properties-genus1}
The elliptic logarithmic form satisfies:

\emph{Periodicity.}
\begin{align}
\eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i + 1, z_j) &= \eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i, z_j) \\
\eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i + \tau, z_j) &= \eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i, z_j) - 2\pi i\,(dz_i - dz_j)
\end{align}
The first equation follows from $\vartheta_1(z+1|\tau) = -\vartheta_1(z|\tau)$ (odd integer quasi-period). The second follows from $\vartheta_1(z+\tau|\tau) = -e^{-\pi i\tau - 2\pi i z}\vartheta_1(z|\tau)$, giving $(\vartheta_1'/\vartheta_1)(z+\tau) = (\vartheta_1'/\vartheta_1)(z) - 2\pi i$. This constant shift of the 1-form is the quasi-periodicity that obstructs $\dfib^{\,2} = 0$ on $E_\tau$.

\emph{Residue.}
\begin{equation}
\operatorname{Res}_{z_i = z_j} \eta_{ij}^{(1)} = 1
\end{equation}

\emph{Modular transformation.} Under $\gamma = \bigl(\begin{smallmatrix}a&b\\c&d\end{smallmatrix}\bigr) \in \operatorname{SL}_2(\mathbb{Z})$, the isomorphism $E_\tau \xrightarrow{\sim} E_{\gamma\tau}$ sends $z \mapsto z/(c\tau+d)$.  Then:
\begin{equation}
\eta_{ij}^{(1)}\!\left(\frac{z_i}{c\tau+d}, \frac{z_j}{c\tau+d} \bigg| \frac{a\tau+b}{c\tau+d}\right)
= \eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i, z_j \mid \tau) + \frac{c\pi i}{c\tau+d}\,(dz_i - dz_j)
\end{equation}
The anomaly term $\frac{c\pi i}{c\tau+d}$ is the source of the modular anomaly of $E_2(\tau)$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}

\emph{Step 1: Periodicity under $z \to z + 1$}

The theta function satisfies $\vartheta_1(z + 1|\tau) = -\vartheta_1(z|\tau)$, hence $\vartheta_1'(z+1|\tau) = -\vartheta_1'(z|\tau)$ and:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\vartheta_1'(z+1|\tau)}{\vartheta_1(z+1|\tau)} = \frac{\vartheta_1'(z|\tau)}{\vartheta_1(z|\tau)}
\end{equation}
Therefore $\eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i + 1, z_j) = \eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i, z_j)$.

\emph{Step 2: Quasi-periodicity under $z \to z + \tau$}

The theta function satisfies:
\begin{equation}
\vartheta_1(z + \tau|\tau) = -e^{-\pi i \tau - 2\pi i z} \vartheta_1(z|\tau)
\end{equation}
Taking $\partial_z \log$ of both sides:
\begin{equation}
\frac{\vartheta_1'(z+\tau|\tau)}{\vartheta_1(z+\tau|\tau)} = \frac{\vartheta_1'(z|\tau)}{\vartheta_1(z|\tau)} - 2\pi i
\end{equation}
Therefore, setting $z = z_i - z_j$:
\begin{equation}
\eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i + \tau, z_j) = \eta_{ij}^{(1)}(z_i, z_j) - 2\pi i\,(dz_i - dz_j)
\end{equation}
The constant shift $-2\pi i$ in the coefficient function produces a constant 1-form shift on configuration space.

\emph{Geometric interpretation.} The quasi-periodic shift $-2\pi i\,(dz_i - dz_j)$ under $z_i \to z_i + \tau$ is the obstruction to $\eta_{ij}^{(1)}$ descending to a well-defined form on $C_n(E_\tau)$.  This shift defines the central extension at genus~1 and is the source of the curvature element $\kappa$ in the bar complex.
\end{proof}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Arnold relations at genus $1$}{Arnold relations at genus 1}}

The genus-$1$ Arnold relation is the $g=1$ case of
Theorem~\ref{thm:quantum-arnold-relations}(b): the correction term
involves pullbacks of $H^1(E_\tau)$ generators.

\begin{theorem}[Genus-1 Arnold relation; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:arnold-genus1}
For three points $z_1, z_2, z_3 \in E_\tau$:
\begin{equation}
\eta_{12}^{(1)} \wedge \eta_{23}^{(1)} + \eta_{23}^{(1)} \wedge \eta_{31}^{(1)}
+ \eta_{31}^{(1)} \wedge \eta_{12}^{(1)} = \sum_{\substack{(i,j,k) \\ \text{cyclic}}} \bigl(\pi_i^*\alpha \wedge \pi_j^*\beta - \pi_i^*\beta \wedge \pi_j^*\alpha\bigr)
\end{equation}
where $\alpha = [dz]$ and $\beta = [d\bar{z}]$ are the generators of
$H^1(E_\tau)$.  See Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:quantum-arnold-relations}}
for the proof and generalization to all genera.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Genus-1 bar complex}

\begin{definition}[Genus-1 bar complex]
\label{def:genus1-bar-complex}
For a chiral algebra $\mathcal{A}$ on $E_\tau$:
\begin{equation}
\bar{B}^{(1)}_p(\mathcal{A}) = \Gamma\left(\overline{C}_{p+1}(E_\tau), 
\mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes (p+1)} \otimes \Omega^p_{\log}\right) \otimes \mathbb{C}[\tau, \bar{\tau}]
\end{equation}

The differential has three components:
\begin{equation}
\Dg{1} = d_{\text{residue}} + d_{\text{elliptic}} + d_{\text{modular}}
\end{equation}

where:
\begin{itemize}
\item $d_{\text{residue}}$: Standard residues at collision divisors (genus-0 part)
\item $d_{\text{elliptic}}$: Elliptic corrections from $\vartheta_1$ and $\wp$
\item $d_{\text{modular}}$: Modular corrections from $E_2(\tau)$
\end{itemize}
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Nilpotency at genus 1; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:genus1-d-squared}
For the Heisenberg algebra on $E_\tau$, we saw in~\S\ref{sec:frame-genus1} that the
fiberwise differential is curved:
\[
\dfib^{\,2} = k \cdot \omega_1.
\]
The total corrected differential is nevertheless strict:
\[
\Dg{1}^{\,2} = 0,
\]
with the curvature appearing as a central correction in $\dfib$, not as a failure
of nilpotence of $\Dg{1}$.

The genus-1 differential satisfies:
\begin{equation}
\Dg{1}^{\,2} = 0
\end{equation}

This requires careful cancellation between:
\begin{enumerate}
\item Genus-0 Arnold relations (exact)
\item Genus-1 corrections (from $E_2$)
\item Holomorphic anomaly compensation
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Write $\Dg{1} = d_r + d_e + d_m$ for the residue, elliptic, and modular components. Expanding $\Dg{1}^{\,2} = (d_r + d_e + d_m)^2$ yields six terms: three diagonal ($d_r^2, d_e^2, d_m^2$) and three anticommutators ($\{d_r, d_e\}, \{d_r, d_m\}, \{d_e, d_m\}$).

\emph{Term 1: $d_r^2 = 0$.}
The residue differential $d_r$ at genus~1 extracts residues at the collision divisors of $\overline{C}_{p+1}(E_\tau)$, exactly as at genus~0. The proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:bar-nilpotency-complete} applies verbatim: disjoint pairs anticommute, overlapping pairs cancel by the Borcherds identity (which is a property of the chiral algebra, not of the curve), and repeated pairs give $\operatorname{Res}_{D_{ij}}^2 = 0$.

\emph{Term 2: $d_e^2 = 0$.}
The elliptic differential $d_e$ acts via the Weierstrass $\zeta$-function $\zeta(z|\tau) = \vartheta_1'(z|\tau)/\vartheta_1(z|\tau)$, which is the genus-1 propagator. The operator $d_e$ is a $\mathcal{D}$-module endomorphism: it multiplies by $\zeta(z_i - z_j|\tau)\,dz_i$ and sums over pairs. Since $d_e$ acts by multiplication by a 1-form on configuration space, $d_e^2$ involves the wedge product $\zeta(z_i - z_j)\,dz_i \wedge \zeta(z_k - z_\ell)\,dz_k$. For $i \neq k$, these anticommute and cancel in the ordered sum. For $i = k$, we get $dz_i \wedge dz_i = 0$. Hence $d_e^2 = 0$.

\emph{Term 3: $d_m^2 = 0$.}
The modular differential $d_m$ is proportional to $\bar{\partial}_\tau = \partial/\partial\bar{\tau}$, acting on the $\mathbb{C}[\tau, \bar{\tau}]$ factor. The non-holomorphic completion
\begin{equation}
\widehat{E}_2(\tau) := E_2(\tau) - \frac{3}{\pi \operatorname{Im}(\tau)}
\end{equation}
transforms as a modular form of weight~2. The modular differential has the form $d_m = f(\tau,\bar{\tau})\,\bar{\partial}_\tau$ for a smooth function~$f$. Since $\bar{\partial}_\tau$ is a flat connection on the trivial bundle over~$\mathbb{H}$, $d_m^2 = f\,\bar{\partial}_\tau(f)\,\bar{\partial}_\tau + f^2\,\bar{\partial}_\tau^2$. But $\bar{\partial}_\tau^2 = 0$ (it is a $(0,1)$-operator in one complex dimension), and $\bar{\partial}_\tau(f) = 0$ because the coefficient function~$f$ arises from the anomaly equation $\bar{\partial}_\tau E_2 = -3/(2\pi i\,\operatorname{Im}(\tau)^2)$, which is itself $\bar{\partial}$-closed. Hence $d_m^2 = 0$.

\emph{Term 4: $\{d_r, d_e\} = 0$.}
Both $d_r$ and $d_e$ act on the spatial variables $(z_1, \ldots, z_{p+1})$ and are $\mathcal{D}_{E_\tau}$-module maps. The anticommutator $d_r \circ d_e + d_e \circ d_r$ extracts residues of the elliptic propagator at collision divisors. Concretely, $\operatorname{Res}_{z_i = z_j}[\zeta(z_i - z_k|\tau)\,dz_i] = \delta_{jk}$ (a constant), so $d_r \circ d_e$ replaces the propagator by its residue. The anticommutator vanishes by the same mechanism as $\{d_{\text{res}}, d_{\text{dR}}\} = 0$ at genus~0: the residue map is compatible with the logarithmic de Rham differential via the residue exact sequence $0 \to \Omega^k \to \Omega^k(\log D) \xrightarrow{\operatorname{Res}} \bigoplus_{D_{ij}} \Omega^{k-1}_{D_{ij}}(\log D') \to 0$.

\emph{Term 5: $\{d_r, d_m\} = 0$.}
The operators $d_r$ and $d_m$ act on different variables: $d_r$ on the spatial coordinates $z_i$ and $d_m$ on the modular parameter~$\bar{\tau}$. Since $d_r$ extracts residues at $z_i = z_j$ (a spatial operation) and $d_m$ differentiates in~$\bar{\tau}$, they commute: $d_r \circ d_m = d_m \circ d_r$. With the sign conventions of the bar complex (both operators have odd total degree), the anticommutator vanishes.

\emph{Term 6: $\{d_e, d_m\} = 0$.}
This is the key genus-1 cancellation. The elliptic propagator $\zeta(z|\tau)$ depends on~$\tau$, so $d_m$ and $d_e$ do not act on independent variables. The anticommutator is:
\[\{d_e, d_m\} = d_m \circ (\zeta \cdot) + (\zeta \cdot) \circ d_m = (\bar{\partial}_\tau \zeta) \cdot\]
We compute $\bar{\partial}_\tau \zeta(z|\tau)$ using the heat equation $\partial_\tau \vartheta_1 = \frac{1}{4\pi i}\partial_z^2 \vartheta_1$. Since $\zeta(z|\tau)$ is holomorphic in~$\tau$ (the non-holomorphic completion enters only through $\widehat{E}_2$, which is part of $d_m$'s definition, not $d_e$'s), we have $\bar{\partial}_\tau \zeta(z|\tau) = 0$. Hence $\{d_e, d_m\} = 0$.

All six terms vanish, so $\Dg{1}^{\,2} = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Universal genus-1 curvature via the modular characteristic; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:genus1-universal-curvature}
Let $\cA$ be a Koszul chiral algebra with modular characteristic $\kappa(\cA)$
\textup{(Theorem~\ref{thm:universal-generating-function})}.  Then the fiberwise
bar differential on the elliptic curve $E_\tau$ satisfies
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:genus1-curved-bar-universal}
\dfib^{\,2} = \kappa(\cA) \cdot \omega_1,
\end{equation}
where $\omega_1 = \frac{i}{2\operatorname{Im}(\tau)}\,dz\wedge d\bar{z}$ is the
Arakelov $(1,1)$-form on $E_\tau$.
The total corrected differential $\Dg{1} = d_0 + F_1(\cA)\cdot d_1$, with
\[
F_1(\cA) = \kappa(\cA)\cdot\lambda_1^{FP} = \frac{\kappa(\cA)}{24},
\]
satisfies $\Dg{1}^{\,2} = 0$ by the same cancellation mechanism as in
Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:genus1-d-squared}}.

Explicit values for the standard landscape:
\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
$\cA$ & $\kappa(\cA)$ & $\kappa(\cA^!)$ & $\kappa + \kappa^!$ & $F_1(\cA)$ \\
\midrule
$\mathcal{H}_\kappa$ & $\kappa$ & $-\kappa$ & $0$ & $\kappa/24$ \\
$\widehat{\mathfrak{sl}}_2$ level $k$ & $\tfrac{3(k+2)}{4}$ & $-\tfrac{3(k+2)}{4}$ & $0$ & $\tfrac{k+2}{32}$ \\
$\mathrm{Vir}_c$ & $c/2$ & $(26-c)/2$ & $13$ & $c/48$ \\
$\mathcal{W}_3$ charge $c$ & $5c/6$ & $5(100-c)/6$ & $250/3$ & $5c/144$ \\
$bc_\lambda$ & $c_{bc}/2$ & --- & --- & $c_{bc}/48$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}
In every case, complementarity $\kappa(\cA) + \kappa(\cA^!) = \mathrm{const}$
holds at genus~$1$, consistent with the genus-$0$ statement of
Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:quantum-complementarity-main}}.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The Heisenberg case is Theorem~\ref{thm:genus1-d-squared}.
For a general Koszul chiral algebra $\cA$, the argument is identical:
the Arnold defect at genus~$1$ is the universal $(1,1)$-form
proportional to $E_2(\tau)$ (Theorem~\ref{thm:arnold-genus1}),
and the coefficient of the defect when contracted with the OPE data
of $\cA$ is exactly the modular characteristic $\kappa(\cA)$,
by the same highest-pole extraction that defines $\kappa(\cA)$ in
Theorem~\ref{thm:universal-generating-function}.
The total differential $\Dg{1}$ absorbs the curvature via the
period correction $F_1(\cA) = \kappa(\cA)/24$, because the
integral $\int_{\overline{\cM}_{1,1}} \psi \cdot \lambda_1 = \lambda_1^{FP} = 1/24$
normalizes the genus-$1$ term in the universal generating function.
The complementarity column follows from Theorem~\ref{thm:quantum-complementarity-main}
evaluated at $g=1$.
\end{proof}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Genus $2$}{Genus 2}}
\label{sec:genus-2-complete}

\subsection{The genus 2 case}

\begin{remark}[Genus 2 vs higher genus]
\label{princ:genus2-special}
Genus~$2$ is the first case with period matrices, spin structures
(16 characteristics), hyperelliptic involution, and the Schottky
problem.  For $g\geq 3$, the full Teichm\"uller theory is needed.
\end{remark}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The moduli space $\mathcal{M}_2$}{The moduli space M-2}}

\begin{definition}[Siegel upper half-space]
\label{def:siegel-h2}
\index{Siegel upper half space|textbf}
The Siegel upper half-space of genus 2 is:
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{H}_2 = \left\{\Omega = \begin{pmatrix} \tau_{11} & \tau_{12} \\ \tau_{12} & \tau_{22} \end{pmatrix} \in M_2(\mathbb{C}) : 
\Omega^T = \Omega, \, \text{Im}(\Omega) > 0\right\}
\end{equation}

where $\text{Im}(\Omega) > 0$ means the imaginary part is positive definite.

\emph{Real dimension}: $\dim_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{H}_2 = 6$ (3 complex parameters)
\emph{Complex dimension}: $\dim_{\mathbb{C}} \mathbb{H}_2 = 3$

The moduli space is:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{M}_2 = \mathbb{H}_2 / Sp_4(\mathbb{Z})
\end{equation}

where $Sp_4(\mathbb{Z})$ is the Siegel modular group.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Period matrix: genus-2 case]
\label{def:period-matrix-g2}
Let $\Sigma_2$ be a genus-2 Riemann surface with canonical homology basis:
\begin{equation}
\{A_1, A_2, B_1, B_2\} \quad \text{with} \quad A_i \cap B_j = \delta_{ij}, \quad A_i \cap A_j = B_i \cap B_j = 0
\end{equation}

Let $\{\omega_1, \omega_2\}$ be the normalized holomorphic 1-forms satisfying:
\begin{equation}
\oint_{A_j} \omega_i = \delta_{ij}
\end{equation}

The period matrix is:
\begin{equation}
\Omega = \begin{pmatrix} 
\oint_{B_1} \omega_1 & \oint_{B_2} \omega_1 \\
\oint_{B_1} \omega_2 & \oint_{B_2} \omega_2
\end{pmatrix}
\in \mathbb{H}_2
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\subsection{Theta functions at genus 2}

\begin{definition}[Genus-2 theta functions]
\label{def:theta-genus2}
For characteristics $\alpha, \beta \in \mathbb{R}^2$:
\begin{equation}
\vartheta[\alpha, \beta](z|\Omega) = \sum_{n \in \mathbb{Z}^2} \exp\left(\pi i (n+\alpha)^T \Omega (n+\alpha) 
+ 2\pi i (n+\alpha)^T (z+\beta)\right)
\end{equation}

where $z \in \mathbb{C}^2$ and $\Omega \in \mathbb{H}_2$.

\emph{Half-period characteristics}: When $\alpha, \beta \in \{0, 1/2\}^2$, we have 16 theta functions.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Odd vs even characteristics {\cite{Fay73}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:odd-even-g2}
At genus 2, the 6 odd characteristics correspond to spin structures with odd fermion parity, and the 10 even characteristics to spin structures with even fermion parity.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Prime form at genus 2}

\begin{definition}[Prime form \texorpdfstring{$E(z,w)$}{E(z,w)} for \texorpdfstring{$g=2$}{g=2}]
\label{def:prime-form-g2}
Choose an odd characteristic $\delta = [\alpha_0, \beta_0]$. The prime form is:
\begin{equation}
E(z,w|\Omega) = \frac{\vartheta[\delta](z-w|\Omega)}{\sqrt{h_\delta(z)} \sqrt{h_\delta(w)}}
\end{equation}

where $h_\delta(z) = \frac{\partial \vartheta[\delta]}{\partial z}(0|\Omega)$ is the gradient of the theta function.

The prime form has the following properties:
\begin{enumerate}
\item $E(z,w)$ is a $(-1/2, -1/2)$ differential in $(z,w)$
\item Simple zero along diagonal: $E(z,w) \sim (z-w)$ as $z \to w$
\item No other zeros on $\Sigma_2 \times \Sigma_2$
\item Independent of choice of odd characteristic $\delta$ (up to sign)
\end{enumerate}
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Computational challenge]
\label{rem:prime-form-computation}
Computing $E(z,w)$ requires normalizing holomorphic differentials, computing the period matrix, evaluating theta functions (convergent for $\text{Im}(\Omega) \gg 0$), and taking gradients.  The procedure is intensive but algorithmic.
\end{remark}

\section{Genus 3: beyond hyperelliptic}
\label{sec:genus-3-complete}

\subsection{The transition at genus 3}

\begin{remark}[Generic vs special curves]
\label{rem:genus3-transition}
At genus $2$, every curve is hyperelliptic. At genus $3$, the hyperelliptic locus $\mathcal{H}_3 \subset \mathcal{M}_3$ has dimension $2g-1 = 5$, while $\dim \mathcal{M}_3 = 3g-3 = 6$, so the generic genus-$3$ curve is \emph{not} hyperelliptic. By Noether's theorem, a generic genus-$3$ curve is a smooth plane quartic, canonically embedded in $\mathbb{P}^2$. For $g \geq 3$, the hyperelliptic locus has codimension $g-2$ in $\mathcal{M}_g$.

For the bar complex, this means genus-$2$ propagators can be expressed via the Weierstrass $\wp$-function, whereas genus~$3$ requires the full theta function machinery on $\operatorname{Jac}(\Sigma_3) = \mathbb{C}^3/\Lambda$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{The moduli space $\mathcal{M}_3$}{The moduli space M-3}}

\begin{definition}[Genus-3 moduli]
\label{def:moduli-g3}
\begin{align}
\dim_{\mathbb{C}} \mathcal{M}_3 &= 3g - 3 = 6 \\
\mathcal{M}_3 &= \mathbb{H}_3 / Sp_6(\mathbb{Z})
\end{align}

where $\mathbb{H}_3$ is the Siegel upper half-space of $3 \times 3$ symmetric matrices.

The period matrix:
\begin{equation}
\Omega = \begin{pmatrix}
\tau_{11} & \tau_{12} & \tau_{13} \\
\tau_{12} & \tau_{22} & \tau_{23} \\
\tau_{13} & \tau_{23} & \tau_{33}
\end{pmatrix} \in \mathbb{H}_3
\end{equation}

has $6$ independent complex entries (since $\Omega^T = \Omega$).
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Theta characteristics at genus 3 {\cite{Fay73}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:theta-g3}
At genus 3, there are $2^{2g} = 2^6 = 64$ theta characteristics.

By Riemann's count, of these 64 characteristics, $2^{g-1}(2^g+1) = 36$ are even and $2^{g-1}(2^g-1) = 28$ are odd. The 28 odd theta characteristics correspond to the 28 bitangent lines of a smooth plane quartic (Riemann's bitangent theorem).
\end{theorem}

\begin{example}[Klein quartic: non-hyperelliptic genus 3]
\label{ex:klein-quartic-g3}
Consider the smooth quartic curve:
\begin{equation}
\Sigma_3: \quad x^3 y + y^3 z + z^3 x = 0 \quad \text{in } \mathbb{P}^2
\end{equation}

This is the \emph{Klein quartic}, which is NOT hyperelliptic.

The Klein quartic has the following properties:
\begin{itemize}
\item Automorphism group: $PSL_2(\mathbb{F}_7)$ (168 elements) - largest for genus 3
\item Canonical embedding: $\Sigma_3 \hookrightarrow \mathbb{P}^2$ (not $\mathbb{P}^1 \times \mathbb{P}^1$)
\item Holomorphic differentials: Generated by $\frac{x dy - y dx}{F_z}$, etc.
\end{itemize}
\end{example}

\section{The genus spectral sequence}
\label{sec:genus-spectral-complete}
\index{spectral sequence!genus|textbf}

The genus-by-genus bar complexes assemble into a single spectral sequence whose
$E_1$~page is the genus-stratified bar cohomology, whose
differentials are the clutching maps along boundary divisors of
$\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$, and whose $E_\infty$~page computes
the full chiral homology.  The collapse page depends on the
family: $E_1$ for Kac--Moody (by CE acyclicity of the
associated graded); $E_2$ for Virasoro and $\mathcal{W}$-algebras
(the DS reduction of the $E_1$-collapsed KM spectral sequence
introduces one additional differential from the BRST operator
$Q_{\mathrm{DS}}$, shifting the collapse by one page;
see Theorem~\ref{thm:ds-koszul-intertwine}).

\subsection{Spectral sequence = genus expansion}

\begin{remark}[Spectral sequence as loop expansion]
\label{princ:ss-genus}
The spectral sequence $E_r^{p,q} \Rightarrow H^{p+q}(\bar{B}(\mathcal{A}))$ organizes contributions by genus: $E_1$ is tree-level (genus~$0$), $E_2$ is one-loop (genus~$1$), and $E_r$ is $(r{-}1)$-loop.
\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Filtration by genus]
\label{def:genus-filtration-complete}
\index{genus filtration|textbf}
Filter the bar complex by genus contribution:
\begin{equation}
F^k \bar{B}(\mathcal{A}) = \bigoplus_{g \geq k} \bar{B}^{(g)}(\mathcal{A})
\end{equation}

This gives:
\begin{equation}
\bar{B}(\mathcal{A}) = F^0 \supset F^1 \supset F^2 \supset \cdots
\end{equation}

The associated graded:
\begin{equation}
\text{Gr}^k_F \bar{B}(\mathcal{A}) = F^k / F^{k+1} = \bar{B}^{(k)}(\mathcal{A})
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Genus filtration as fundamental parameter]
\label{rem:genus-fundamental-parameter}
\index{genus filtration!as fundamental parameter}
Each genus level carries distinct content: $g=0$ (classical Koszul
duality), $g=1$ (curvature $\dfib^{\,2}=\kappa\cdot\omega_1$,
central extension), $g\geq 2$ ($H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$
contributions via Feynman transform, growth controlled by
$\operatorname{Vol}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)\sim(2g)!$).
\end{remark}

\begin{theorem}[\texorpdfstring{$E_1$}{E1} page explicit; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:e1-page-complete}
The $E_1$ page of the genus spectral sequence is:
\begin{equation}
E_1^{p,q,g} = H^q\left(\bar{B}^p(\Sigma_g), d^{(g)}_{\mathrm{internal}}\right)
\end{equation}

\emph{Small genus:}
\begin{align}
E_1^{*,*,0} &= H^*(\overline{C}_*(\mathbb{P}^1), \mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes *}) \quad \text{(genus 0)} \\
E_1^{*,*,1} &= H^*(\overline{C}_*(E_\tau), \mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes *}) \otimes \mathbb{C}[\tau, \bar{\tau}] \quad \text{(genus 1)} \\
E_1^{*,*,2} &= H^*(\overline{C}_*(\Sigma_2), \mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes *}) \otimes \mathbb{C}[\Omega, \bar{\Omega}] \quad \text{(genus 2)}
\end{align}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By Definition~\ref{def:genus-filtration-complete}, the genus filtration $F^k\bar{B}(\mathcal{A}) = \bigoplus_{g \geq k} \bar{B}^{(g)}(\mathcal{A})$ is a descending filtration of cochain complexes.  The associated spectral sequence has:
\[
E_0^{p,q,g} = \bar{B}^{p,q}(\Sigma_g, \mathcal{A}) = \Gamma\bigl(\overline{C}_{p+1}(\Sigma_g),\, \mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes(p+1)} \otimes \Omega^q_{\log}\bigr)
\]
with $d_0 = d^{(g)}_{\mathrm{internal}}$, the component of the bar differential that preserves the genus (i.e., the collision residue part $d^{(0)}$ of Theorem~\ref{thm:concrete-quantum-differential}).  The $E_1$ page is then:
\[
E_1^{p,q,g} = H^q\bigl(\bar{B}^p(\Sigma_g),\, d^{(0)}\bigr)
\]
which computes the cohomology of the degree-$p$ bar complex on a \emph{fixed} curve~$\Sigma_g$ with respect to the genus-0 (collision) part of the differential.

For genus~0: $\Sigma_0 = \mathbb{P}^1$ has no moduli, so $E_1^{*,*,0} = H^*(\overline{C}_*(\mathbb{P}^1), \mathcal{A}^{\boxtimes *})$, computed using the Arnold relations on $\mathbb{P}^1$.

For genus~1: $\Sigma_1 = E_\tau$ is an elliptic curve parameterized by $\tau \in \mathbb{H}$.  The internal cohomology depends on $\tau$ through the elliptic propagator $\eta_{12}^{(1)} = d\log\theta_1(z_{12}|\tau)$, so $E_1^{*,*,1}$ carries a $\mathbb{C}[\tau,\bar{\tau}]$-module structure.

For genus~2: $\Sigma_2$ is parameterized by the period matrix $\Omega = (\Omega_{ij})_{1 \leq i,j \leq 2} \in \mathbb{H}_2$ (the Siegel upper half-space of symmetric $2 \times 2$ matrices with positive-definite imaginary part), a $3$-complex-dimensional space.  The genus-2 propagator $\eta_{12}^{(2)} = d\log E(z_1, z_2 | \Omega)$ (where $E$ is the prime form on $\Sigma_2$) depends on $\Omega$ through the theta function, so $E_1^{*,*,2}$ carries a $\mathbb{C}[\Omega_{ij}, \bar{\Omega}_{ij}]$-module structure.  The computation is otherwise parallel to genus~1: internal cohomology is computed on a fixed $\Sigma_2$ using the genus-2 Arnold--Orlik--Solomon relations (which hold with the same combinatorial structure but with genus-2 propagators), and the moduli dependence enters through the propagator's dependence on~$\Omega$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[\texorpdfstring{$E_2$}{E2} page structure; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:e2-page-complete}
The $E_2$ page computes:
\begin{equation}
E_2^{p,q,g} = H^p\left(\mathcal{M}_g, \underline{H}^q(\bar{B}^{(g)})\right)
\end{equation}

where $\underline{H}^q$ is the local system of cohomology groups over moduli space.

\emph{Explicit for genus~$1$.}
\begin{equation}
E_2^{p,q,1} = H^p(\mathcal{M}_1, \mathcal{M}_k \otimes H^q) = \bigoplus_{k} \mathcal{M}_k \otimes H^{q}
\end{equation}

where $\mathcal{M}_k$ are modular forms of weight $k$.

The differential $d_2: E_2^{p,q} \to E_2^{p+2,q-1}$ is the Kodaira--Spencer map.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
At fixed genus~$g$, the universal configuration space
$\overline{\mathcal{C}}_{g,n} \to \mathcal{M}_g$ (relative FM
compactification over moduli) gives a Leray spectral sequence
with $d_1: E_1^{p,q,g} \to E_1^{p+1,q,g}$ (the moduli-space differential
at fixed genus).  This Leray $d_1$ acts by differentiation with respect
to moduli parameters.  The $E_2$ page is:
\[
E_2^{p,q,g} = H^p(\mathcal{M}_g,\, \underline{H}^q(\bar{B}^{(g)}))
\]
where $\underline{H}^q$ is the local system of fiber cohomologies
over~$\mathcal{M}_g$.

Separately, the genus expansion $d_{\mathrm{total}} = \sum_g \hbar^{2g-2} d_g$
gives a \emph{genus spectral sequence} whose $d_1$ shifts genus by~$+1$
(the genus-$1$ correction $d^{(1)}$ from
Theorem~\ref{thm:concrete-quantum-differential}).  The two spectral
sequences interact via the total filtration: the Leray spectral
sequence operates within each genus stratum, while the genus spectral
sequence connects different strata.

\emph{Genus-1 computation.}
At genus~1, $\mathcal{M}_1 = \mathbb{H}/SL_2(\mathbb{Z})$ and the local systems are modular forms.  The quasi-modularity of $E_2(\tau)$ (which appears in $d_{\mathrm{dR}}\eta_{12}^{(1)} = 2\pi i\, \omega_\tau$, cf.\ Example~\ref{ex:heisenberg-genus-1}) implies that $d_1$ is given by the Ramanujan-Serre derivative $\theta_k = q\frac{d}{dq} - \frac{k}{12}E_2$, which maps modular forms of weight~$k$ to quasi-modular forms of weight~$k+2$.  The $E_2$ page therefore decomposes into spaces of (quasi-)modular forms of weight~$k$ tensored with the internal cohomology~$H^q$.

\emph{Identification of $d_2$.}
The $d_2$ differential maps $E_2^{p,q} \to E_2^{p+2,q-1}$ and is identified with the Kodaira--Spencer map $\kappa: T_{\mathcal{M}_g} \to R^1\pi_* T_{\Sigma_g/\mathcal{M}_g}$, which measures how the complex structure of~$\Sigma_g$ deforms as we move in moduli space.  At genus~1, this is the classical identification of the Kodaira--Spencer class with $\partial/\partial\tau$, and $d_2$ acts by differentiating with respect to $\tau$ (modulo the quasi-modular correction from~$E_2$).
\end{proof}


% ================================================================
% SECTION 4.13: MODULI SPACE COHOMOLOGY AND QUANTUM OBSTRUCTION
% ================================================================

\section{Moduli space cohomology and quantum obstructions}
\label{sec:moduli-cohomology-quantum}

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Cohomology of $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$}{Cohomology of M-g,n}}

\begin{theorem}[Mumford--Morita--Miller classes {\cite{Mumford83}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:mmm-classes}
The cohomology ring $H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}, \mathbb{Q})$ is generated by:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \emph{Tautological classes:}
\begin{itemize}
\item $\lambda_i \in H^{2i}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n})$ (Chern classes of Hodge bundle)
\item $\psi_i \in H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n})$ (first Chern classes of cotangent lines at marked points)
\item $[\Delta_I] \in H^{2}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n})$ (boundary divisor classes, codimension~$1$)
\end{itemize}

\item \emph{Generators in low genus:}
\begin{align}
H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{0,n}) &= \mathbb{Q}[\psi_1, \ldots, \psi_n] / (\text{relations})\\
H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}) &= \mathbb{Q}[\lambda_1] / (\lambda_1^2)\\
H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g) &\supset \mathbb{Q}[\lambda_1, \ldots, \lambda_g] \text{ for } g \geq 2
\end{align}
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}[Provenance and citation]
This theorem is imported and treated as \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere. Standard
descriptions of tautological generators and low-genus cohomology structure for
$\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$ are classical in the moduli-space literature;
see~\cite{Mumford83}.
\end{remark}

\begin{definition}[Hodge bundle]
\label{def:hodge-bundle}
\index{Hodge bundle|textbf}
The \emph{Hodge bundle} $\mathbb{E} \to \overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}$ is the rank-$g$ vector bundle whose fiber over $[(\Sigma_g; p_1, \ldots, p_n)]$ is:
\begin{equation}
\mathbb{E}_{[\Sigma_g]} = H^0(\Sigma_g, \Omega^1_{\Sigma_g})
\end{equation}
the space of holomorphic differentials.

The Chern classes:
\begin{equation}
\lambda_i = c_i(\mathbb{E}) \in H^{2i}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n}, \mathbb{Q})
\end{equation}
are the \emph{$\lambda$-classes}. (The \emph{Mumford--Morita--Miller classes} $\kappa_i = \pi_*(\psi_{n+1}^{i+1}) \in H^{2i}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,n})$ are related but distinct: they are pushforwards of powers of the universal cotangent class.)
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Faber--Pandharipande $\lambda_g$ formula {\cite{FP03}}; \ClaimStatusProvedElsewhere]
\label{thm:mumford-formula}
\index{Faber--Pandharipande formula}
The top $\lambda$-class, paired with the appropriate $\psi$-class, evaluates to:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:faber-pandharipande}
\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,1}} \psi_1^{2g-2}\,\lambda_g = \frac{2^{2g-1}-1}{2^{2g-1}} \cdot \frac{|B_{2g}|}{(2g)!}
\end{equation}
where $B_{2g}$ are Bernoulli numbers~\cite{FP03}.  The integrand has degree $(2g-2)+g = 3g-2 = \dim\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{g,1}$.  At $g = 1$: $\frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{1/6}{2} = \frac{1}{24}$; at $g = 2$: $\frac{7}{8} \cdot \frac{1/30}{24} = \frac{7}{5760}$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}[Imported-proof dependency tags]
\emph{Dependency tags.}
\begin{enumerate}[label=(D\arabic*)]
\item The class-theoretic setup is provided by
Theorem~\ref{thm:mmm-classes} and Definition~\ref{def:hodge-bundle}.
\item The closed-form Bernoulli expression is imported from the external
tautological-ring literature (see~\cite{Mumford83}).
\item In this manuscript, the formula is used as input to obstruction and
quantum-correction bookkeeping (e.g. Theorem~\ref{thm:obstruction-general}).
\end{enumerate}
\emph{Provenance note.}
This claim is used in this manuscript as an external theorem, proved in
\cite{Mumford83}. It is not reproved internally here.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Quantum obstructions as cohomology classes}

\begin{theorem}[Obstruction theory for quantum corrections; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:obstruction-quantum}
For a chiral algebra $\mathcal{A}$ and deformation parameter $t$, the obstruction to extending from genus $g-1$ to genus $g$ lies in:
\begin{equation}
\text{Obs}^{(g)}(\mathcal{A}) \in H^2(\bar{B}_g(\mathcal{A}), \mathcal{Z}(\mathcal{A}))
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal{Z}(\mathcal{A})$ is the center of $\mathcal{A}$ viewed as a sheaf on $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$.

Explicitly, $\text{Obs}^{(1)}(\mathcal{A})$ captures central extensions and $\text{Obs}^{(g)}(\mathcal{A})$ for $g \geq 2$ captures higher genus anomalies.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Consider the Leray spectral sequence for the bar complex sheaf on $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$:
\begin{equation}
E_2^{p,q} = H^p(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, \mathcal{H}^q(\bar{B}(\mathcal{A}))) \Rightarrow H^{p+q}(\bar{B}^{\text{global}}(\mathcal{A}))
\end{equation}
The obstruction to extending from genus $g-1$ to genus $g$ is the $d_2$ differential:
\begin{equation}
d_2: E_2^{0,1} \to E_2^{2,0}
\end{equation}
which measures the failure of local sections of the bar complex to extend globally over $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$.
For central elements, $\mathcal{H}^0(\bar{B}(\mathcal{A})) = \mathcal{Z}(\mathcal{A})$ (the center acts trivially on the bar differential), so the obstruction lands in $E_2^{2,0} = H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, \mathcal{Z}(\mathcal{A}))$. The two cases follow: at $g=1$, $H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}, \mathbb{C}) \cong \mathbb{C}$ captures the central extension; for $g \geq 3$, $H^2(\mathcal{M}_g) \cong \mathbb{Q}$ by Harer stability, while $H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$ has additional boundary classes (e.g., $\dim H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2) = 3$).
\end{proof}

\subsection{Explicit computation for small genus}

\begin{example}[Genus-1 obstruction]
\label{ex:genus-1-obstruction-complete}
For $g=1$, the coarse moduli space $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}$ is isomorphic to $\mathbb{P}^1$ (the $j$-line, compactified by the nodal rational curve at $j = \infty$); as a Deligne--Mumford stack it is $[\overline{\mathcal{H}}/\mathrm{SL}_2(\mathbb{Z})]$ with $\dim_{\mathbb{C}} = 1$.  The Hodge class $\lambda = c_1(\mathbb{E})$ generates $\mathrm{Pic}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1})_{\mathbb{Q}}$ with $\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}} \lambda = 1/24$.

The cohomology is:
\begin{equation}
H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}) = \mathbb{Q}[\lambda] / (\lambda^2) \cong \mathbb{Q} \oplus \mathbb{Q}\lambda
\end{equation}

For the Heisenberg algebra $\mathcal{H}_\kappa$, the obstruction to genus-1 extension defines a class in $H^2$:
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{obs}_1 = \kappa \cdot \lambda_1 \in H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}, \mathbb{C}) \cong \mathbb{C}
\end{equation}
(Note: $H^1(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}) = 0$ since the Poincar\'e polynomial $1 + t^2$ has no odd-degree terms.)

The \emph{central charge} is the numerical invariant:
\begin{equation}
\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}} \mathrm{obs}_1 = \kappa \int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}} \lambda_1 = \frac{\kappa}{24}
\end{equation}
\end{example}

\begin{example}[Genus 2 obstruction]
\label{ex:genus-2-obstruction}
For $g=2$, the moduli space $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2$ has dimension 3. The cohomology begins:
\begin{equation}
H^1(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2) = 0, \quad H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2) \cong \mathbb{Q}^{\oplus 3}
\end{equation}

Genus-2 quantum corrections for a chiral algebra $\mathcal{A}$ give classes:
\begin{equation}
[c_2] \in H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2, \mathcal{Z}(\mathcal{A}))
\end{equation}

For W-algebras, these involve \emph{screening charges} and \emph{higher central charges}.
\end{example}

% ================================================================
% PATCH 022: OBSTRUCTION CLASS COMPUTATION - EXPLICIT FORMULAS
% ================================================================

\section{Obstruction classes: explicit computation for all examples}
\label{sec:obstruction-explicit}

The obstruction class $\text{obs}_k \in H^2(B_g, Z(\mathcal{A}))$ admits explicit formulas for all key examples.

\subsection{Recollection: obstruction theory framework}
\label{subsec:obstruction-framework-recall}

\begin{definition}[Genus-\texorpdfstring{$g$}{g} obstruction class]\label{def:genus-g-obstruction}
\index{obstruction class|textbf}
\index{obstruction class!as projection of Theta}
The \emph{genus-$g$ obstruction class} of a modular Koszul chiral
algebra~$\cA$ is the genus-$g$, arity-$0$ projection of the
scalar level of the shadow Postnikov tower:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:obs-g-projection}
\mathrm{obs}_g(\cA)
\;:=\;
\Theta_{\cA}^{\leq 2}\big|_{g,\,n=0}
\;=\;
\kappa(\cA) \cdot \lambda_g
\;\in\;
H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, \mathcal{Z}(\cA)).
\end{equation}
By genus universality
\textup{(}Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:genus-universality})},
$\mathrm{obs}_g(\cA) = \kappa(\cA) \cdot \lambda_g$.

\emph{Analytic interpretation.}
The class $\mathrm{obs}_g$ measures the failure of the fiberwise
bar differential to square to zero:
$\dfib^{\,2} = \kappa(\cA) \cdot \omega_g$ on a fixed curve~$\Sigma_g$
\textup{(}Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:quantum-arnold-relations})}.
The bar-degree-$2$ obstruction maps to $H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$
under the Leray spectral sequence for
$\overline{\mathcal{C}}_{g,n} \to \overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$,
landing on $\lambda_g \in H^{2g}$.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Obstruction formula; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:obstruction-general}
\textup{[Regime: curved-central
\textup{(}Convention~\textup{\ref{conv:regime-tags})}.]}

The genus-$g$ obstruction is computed by:
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{obs}_g = \sum_{\substack{g_1+g_2=g \\ g_1, g_2 \geq 1}} d_{g_1} \circ d_{g_2}
\end{equation}
where $d_{g'}$ is the genus-$g'$ component of the bar differential.  The condition $d_{\mathrm{total}}^2 = 0$ at order $\hbar^{2g-4}$ requires $[d_0, d_g] + \mathrm{obs}_g = 0$, so $d_g$ exists if and only if $\mathrm{obs}_g$ is a $d_0$-coboundary. The obstruction class $[\mathrm{obs}_g] \in H^2(\bar{B}_0(\mathcal{A}), Z(\mathcal{A}))$ is well-defined (independent of choices) and lands in the center by the argument in Step~4 below.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof of formula]

\emph{Step 1: Genus stratification of the differential.}

The full bar differential decomposes as:
\[d_{\text{total}} = \sum_{g=0}^{\infty} \hbar^{2g-2} d_g\]
(We use the closed-string convention $\hbar^{2g-2}$; the Feynman-diagram convention in \S\ref{sec:feynman_genus_g} uses $\hbar^{g-1}$, corresponding to $\hbar_{\text{there}} = \hbar_{\text{here}}^2$.)

Each $d_g$ involves integration over $g$-loop configuration spaces:
\[d_g = \sum_{n \geq 1} \int_{\overline{C}_n^{(g)}(X)} \text{Res}_{D} \circ \eta_g\]

\emph{Step~2: Squaring the differential.}

Compute $d_{\mathrm{total}}^2$:
\begin{align*}
d_{\text{total}}^2 &= \left(\sum_{g} \hbar^{2g-2} d_g\right)^2 \\
&= \sum_{g_1, g_2} \hbar^{2(g_1+g_2)-4} d_{g_1} \circ d_{g_2}
\end{align*}

At genus $g$ (i.e., coefficient of $\hbar^{2g-4}$), the condition $d_{\text{total}}^2 = 0$ gives:
\[\sum_{\substack{g_1 + g_2 = g \\ g_1, g_2 \geq 0}} d_{g_1} d_{g_2} = 0\]
Expanding: $d_0 d_g + d_1 d_{g-1} + \cdots + d_{g-1} d_1 + d_g d_0 = 0$.

\emph{Step 3: Arnold relations at genus zero.}

At genus zero, $d_0^2 = 0$ by the Arnold relations (Theorem~\ref{thm:arnold-three}).
For $g \geq 1$, the genus-$g$ condition $\sum_{g_1+g_2=g} d_{g_1}d_{g_2} = 0$ becomes $[d_0, d_g] = -\sum_{\substack{g_1+g_2=g \\ g_1,g_2 \geq 1}} d_{g_1}d_{g_2}$.  The obstruction comes from the right-hand side: the lower-genus differentials determine a constraint on $d_g$.

\emph{Step 4: Central elements.}

The obstruction $\operatorname{obs}_g = \sum_{\substack{g_1+g_2=g \\ g_1,g_2 \geq 1}} d_{g_1} \circ d_{g_2}$ lands in the center $Z(\mathcal{A})$.  For centrality, we use two facts.  First, $[d_0, \operatorname{obs}_g] = 0$ by the genus-$(g{+}1)$ condition from Step~2, so $\operatorname{obs}_g$ is a $d_0$-cocycle in $\mathrm{End}(\bar{B}(\mathcal{A}))$ and defines a class in $H^0(\mathrm{End}(\bar{B}(\mathcal{A})), [d_0, -])$.  Second, each $d_{g_i}$ ($g_i \geq 1$) is constructed from period integrals $\oint_{\gamma_k} \omega$ over homology cycles $\gamma_k \subset \Sigma_g$.  These are \emph{global} operations on the curve, while the chiral bracket $\mu: \mathcal{A} \boxtimes \mathcal{A} \to \Delta_*\mathcal{A}$ is \emph{local} (supported on the diagonal).  By the locality axiom of chiral algebras, global operations on disjoint supports commute with local operations: $[\oint_{\gamma_k}, \mu(a, -)] = 0$ for any $a \in \mathcal{A}$, provided $\gamma_k$ does not pass through the collision locus.  Therefore $\operatorname{obs}_g$ commutes with all chiral products, hence is central.

\emph{Step 5: Moduli space integration.}

The genus-$g$ correction form $\omega_g$ appears through period integrals:
\[\omega_g = \int_{\gamma \in H_1(\Sigma_g)} \eta \wedge \bar{\eta}\]

Combining with Step 2 gives the stated formula.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Example 1: Heisenberg algebra, level shift obstruction}
\label{subsec:heisenberg-obstruction}

\begin{theorem}[Heisenberg obstruction at genus \texorpdfstring{$g$}{g}; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:heisenberg-obs}
For the Heisenberg vertex algebra $\mathcal{H}_\kappa$ at level $\kappa$, the 
genus-$g$ obstruction is:
\begin{equation}
\text{obs}_g^{\mathcal{H}} = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g \in H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, \mathbb{C})
\end{equation}
where $\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E})$ is the top Chern class of the Hodge bundle.

Explicitly:
\begin{itemize}
\item $g=1$: $\text{obs}_1 = \kappa \cdot [\tau]$ where $[\tau] \in H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1)$
\item $g=2$: $\text{obs}_2 = \kappa \cdot \lambda_2 = \kappa \cdot c_2(\mathbb{E})$
\item $g \geq 3$: $\text{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$
\end{itemize}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}

\emph{Step 1: Heisenberg structure.}

The Heisenberg algebra has generators $a_n$ with:
\[[a_m, a_n] = \kappa \cdot m \cdot \delta_{m+n,0} \cdot c\]
where $c$ is the central element.

\emph{Step 2: Bar differential at genus $g$.}

For $a_m \in \mathcal{H}_\kappa$, the genus-$g$ bar differential is:
\begin{align}
d_g(a_m) &= \sum_{k=-\infty}^{\infty} \int_{\overline{C}_2^{(g)}} a_k \otimes a_{m-k} 
\otimes \eta_{12}^{(g)} \\
&= \sum_{k} \int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g} a_k \otimes a_{m-k} \otimes 
\left(\int_{\Sigma_g} \text{d}\log\theta_1(z_{12}; \Omega_g)\right)
\end{align}

\emph{Step 3: Squaring the fiberwise differential.}

Compute $\dfib^{\,2}(a_m)$ on a fixed $\Sigma_g$:
\begin{align}
\dfib^{\,2}(a_m) &= \dfib\!\left(\sum_k \int_{\mathcal{M}_g} a_k \otimes a_{m-k} \otimes \omega_g\right) \\
&= \sum_{k_1, k_2} \int_{\mathcal{M}_g} [a_{k_1}, a_{k_2}] \otimes a_{m-k_1-k_2}
\otimes \omega_g^2
\end{align}

\emph{Step 4: Commutator evaluation.}

Using $[a_{k_1}, a_{k_2}] = \kappa \cdot k_1 \cdot \delta_{k_1 + k_2, 0} \cdot c$,
the Kronecker delta forces $k_2 = -k_1$, so
$a_{m - k_1 - k_2} = a_m$.  The remaining sum
$\sum_{k} k$ over mode indices is evaluated geometrically:
in the D-module formulation, the mode sum computes the
first Chern class of the direct image sheaf $R^0\pi_*\omega_{\mathcal{C}/\mathcal{M}_g}$
via the Quillen determinant anomaly formula~\cite{Quillen85}.  The result is:
\begin{equation}
\dfib^{\,2}(a_m) = \kappa \cdot a_m \otimes \lambda_g
\end{equation}
where $\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E}) \in H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$.

\emph{Step 5: Evaluation of the obstruction class.}

At genus~$1$: the moduli space $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1$ has complex dimension~$1$, so $\lambda_1 = c_1(\mathbb{E})$ is a top-degree class, and the integral $\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1} \lambda_1 = 1/24$ (the degree of the Hodge bundle).

At genus~$g \geq 2$: $\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E})$ has degree~$g < 3g{-}3 = \dim_{\mathbb{C}}\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$, so the obstruction class $\mathrm{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$ is a non-trivial class in $H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$ but is \emph{not} a top-degree class.  Its numerical evaluation requires pairing with a complementary tautological class of degree~$2g{-}3$; the relevant intersection numbers are computable from the Faber conjectures (proved by Faber--Pandharipande) involving Bernoulli numbers.

\emph{Step 6: Obstruction class.}

Therefore:
\[\text{obs}_g^{\mathcal{H}} = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g\]

This is a central element (proportional to $c$), confirming the consistency.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Physical interpretation: anomaly]\label{rem:heisenberg-anomaly}
The obstruction class $\text{obs}_g$ is the conformal anomaly at genus $g$.  The Bernoulli numbers $B_{2g}$ in Mumford's formula govern both the arithmetic of $\zeta(2g)$ and the geometry of $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Example 2: Kac--Moody algebras, level and dual Coxeter number}
\label{subsec:kac-moody-obstruction}

\begin{theorem}[Kac--Moody obstruction at genus \texorpdfstring{$g$}{g}; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:kac-moody-obs}
For the affine Kac--Moody vertex algebra $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k$ at level $k \neq -h^\vee$,
the genus-$g$ obstruction is:
\begin{equation}
\text{obs}_g^{\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}} = \frac{(k + h^\vee) \cdot
\text{dim}(\mathfrak{g})}{2h^\vee} \cdot \lambda_g
\end{equation}
where $h^\vee$ is the dual Coxeter number of $\mathfrak{g}$ and $\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E})$
is the top Chern class of the Hodge bundle on $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$.

For specific Lie algebras:
\begin{align}
\mathfrak{g} = \mathfrak{sl}_2: \quad \text{obs}_g &= \frac{3(k+2)}{4} \lambda_g \\
\mathfrak{g} = \mathfrak{sl}_3: \quad \text{obs}_g &= \frac{4(k+3)}{3} \lambda_g \\
\mathfrak{g} = E_8: \quad \text{obs}_g &= \frac{62(k+30)}{15} \lambda_g
\end{align}

At the critical level $k = -h^\vee$, the obstruction vanishes for all $g$, consistent with
$d^2 = 0$ on the bar complex (Proposition~\ref{prop:km-bar-curvature}(i)).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}

\emph{Step 1: Genus-zero curvature.}

By Proposition~\ref{prop:km-bar-curvature}, the bar complex
of $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k$ has curvature:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:km-curvature-recall}
m_0 = \frac{k + h^\vee}{2h^\vee} \cdot \kappa
\end{equation}
where $\kappa = \sum_a J^a \otimes J_a$ is the Casimir element.  This curvature arises
because squaring the bar differential on degree-2 elements produces:
\[d^2(J^a \boxtimes J^b \cdot \eta_{12}) = (k + h^\vee) \cdot
\bigl(\text{adjoint Casimir contribution}\bigr)\]
The factor $h^\vee$ comes from the contraction
$\sum_c f^{ac}{}_d f^{bc}{}_e = 2h^\vee \cdot (J^a, J^b)$
(the Casimir eigenvalue on the adjoint representation in the normalization where
long roots have squared length 2).

\emph{Step 2: Extension to genus $g$.}

By the general obstruction formula (Theorem~\ref{thm:obstruction-general}), the genus-$g$
obstruction is:
\[\text{obs}_g = \int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g} \omega_g \otimes m_0\]
For $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k$, the curvature element $m_0$ from Step~1 acts on each of the $\dim(\mathfrak{g})$ current generators.
The integration over $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$ produces the Hodge class $\lambda_g$
by the same mechanism as in the Heisenberg case (Theorem~\ref{thm:heisenberg-obs}, Step~5):
the integral $\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g} \omega_g^2 = \lambda_g$ via Mumford's formula.

\emph{Step 3: Assembling the formula.}

Each generator $J^a$ contributes a copy of the curvature $m_0$.  Summing over
$a = 1, \ldots, \dim(\mathfrak{g})$ and noting that $\kappa$ evaluated on the trace gives
$\sum_a (J^a, J_a) = \dim(\mathfrak{g})$:
\begin{align}
\text{obs}_g &= \dim(\mathfrak{g}) \cdot m_0 \cdot \lambda_g \\
&= \dim(\mathfrak{g}) \cdot \frac{k+h^\vee}{2h^\vee} \cdot \lambda_g \\
&= \frac{(k+h^\vee)\dim(\mathfrak{g})}{2h^\vee} \cdot \lambda_g
\end{align}

\emph{Step 4: Consistency checks.}

\emph{Critical level}: At $k = -h^\vee$, $\text{obs}_g = 0$ for all $g$. This is consistent
with $d^2 = 0$ on the bar complex at critical level (Proposition~\ref{prop:km-bar-curvature}(i)),
which reflects the enlarged center of $V_{-h^\vee}(\mathfrak{g})$.

\emph{Heisenberg limit}: Taking $\mathfrak{g}$ abelian (so $h^\vee = 0$ requires care),
we instead note that for the Heisenberg algebra the structure constants vanish ($f^{abc} = 0$),
so the Casimir correction is absent.  The obstruction reduces to
$\text{obs}_g = k \cdot \lambda_g$, in agreement with Theorem~\ref{thm:heisenberg-obs}.

\emph{Koszul duality}: Under the Feigin--Frenkel involution $k \mapsto -k-2h^\vee$, the
obstruction transforms as:
\[\text{obs}_g^{\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_{-k-2h^\vee}} =
\frac{(-k-2h^\vee+h^\vee)\dim(\mathfrak{g})}{2h^\vee} \cdot \lambda_g
= \frac{-(k+h^\vee)\dim(\mathfrak{g})}{2h^\vee} \cdot \lambda_g
= -\text{obs}_g^{\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k}\]
The sign reversal is consistent with Main~Theorem~C (Theorem~\ref{thm:quantum-complementarity-main}; the Kodaira--Spencer construction of Theorem~\ref{thm:kodaira-spencer-chiral-complete} provides the geometric mechanism):
what $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k$ sees as obstruction, $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_{-k-2h^\vee}$
sees as the negative obstruction, confirming deformation-obstruction complementarity.

\emph{Step 5: Verification for $\mathfrak{sl}_2$.}

For $\mathfrak{sl}_2$: $\dim(\mathfrak{sl}_2) = 3$, $h^\vee = 2$,
$c_{\mathfrak{sl}_2, k} = 3k/(k+2)$.

The obstruction is:
\[\text{obs}_g = \frac{(k+2) \cdot 3}{2 \cdot 2} \lambda_g = \frac{3(k+2)}{4} \lambda_g\]

At genus 1 with $k=1$:
\[\text{obs}_1 = \frac{3 \cdot 3}{4} \lambda_1 = \frac{9}{4} \lambda_1\]

Numerically:
\[\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1} \lambda_1 = \frac{1}{24}\]

Therefore
\[\int_{\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1} \text{obs}_1 = \frac{9}{4} \cdot \frac{1}{24}
= \frac{3}{32}\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Level-rank duality]\label{rem:level-rank-obstruction}
The obstruction formula exhibits level-rank duality explicitly. For $\mathfrak{sl}_N$
at level $k$ (with $h^\vee(\mathfrak{sl}_N) = N$, $\dim(\mathfrak{sl}_N) = N^2-1$):
\[\text{obs}_g^{\widehat{\mathfrak{sl}}_N(k)} =
\frac{(k+N) \cdot (N^2-1)}{2N} \cdot \lambda_g\]

Under level-rank duality $\mathfrak{sl}_N(k) \leftrightarrow \mathfrak{sl}_k(N)$:
\[\text{obs}_g^{\widehat{\mathfrak{sl}}_k(N)} =
\frac{(N+k) \cdot (k^2-1)}{2k} \cdot \lambda_g\]

The common factor $(N+k)$ is symmetric, while the remaining factors
$(N^2-1)/N$ and $(k^2-1)/k$ are exchanged under $N \leftrightarrow k$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Example 3: $\mathcal{W}$-algebras, central charge dependence}
\label{subsec:w-algebra-obstruction}

\begin{theorem}[\texorpdfstring{$W_3$}{W3} fiberwise obstruction; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:w3-obstruction}
For the $W_3$ algebra with generators $T$ (weight~$2$) and $W$
(weight~$3$) at central charge~$c$:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\alph*)}]
\item The fiberwise curvature is
$\dfib^{\,2} = \operatorname{diag}(c/2,\, c/3) \otimes \omega_g$,
with both channels using the standard Hodge
bundle~$\mathbb{E} = R^0\pi_*\omega_\pi$
\textup{(}Remark~\textup{\ref{rem:propagator-weight-universality}}\textup{)}.
\item At genus~$1$,
$\mathrm{obs}_1^{W_3} = \frac{5c}{6} \cdot \lambda_1$
where $\kappa(\mathcal{W}_3^k) = c/2 + c/3 = 5c/6$.
\item Scalar saturation \textup{(}$\dim H^2_{\mathrm{cyc}} = 1$\textup{)}
fixes the $H^2$ direction to~$\eta$, but does not determine
the $\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g$ class: the Kuranishi map vanishes
by parity, so the MC equation places no constraint on which
class in $H^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$ appears at genus~$g$.
Whether the bar construction produces $\lambda_g$ at $g \geq 2$
for multi-weight algebras is
Open Problem~\textup{\ref{op:multi-generator-universality}}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The bar differential at genus~$g$ uses the propagator
$\eta_{ij}^{(g)} = \partial_{z_i}\log E(z_i,z_j) +
{}$\textup{(}period correction\textup{)}, which has weight~$1$
for both channels (Remark~\ref{rem:propagator-weight-universality}).
The $B$-cycle quasi-periodicity produces the Arakelov
form $\omega_g = c_1(\mathbb{E})$ identically for both:
$\dfib^{\,2}(T) = (c/2) \cdot T \otimes \omega_g$,
$\dfib^{\,2}(W) = (c/3) \cdot W \otimes \omega_g$.
The conformal weights enter through the OPE coefficients
(numbers), not through the Hodge bundle.
At genus~$1$, $H^2(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_{1,1}) \cong
\mathbb{C}$ gives $\mathrm{obs}_1 = (5c/6)\,\lambda_1$.
Part~(c): $\dim H^2_{\mathrm{cyc}}(\mathcal{W}_3) = 1$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:cyclic-rigidity-generic}) forces the
minimal-model MC element to $\Theta^{\min} = \eta
\otimes \Omega$ for some $\Omega \in \widehat{\Gmod}$.
The Kuranishi map vanishes by parity ($s^{-1}\eta$ odd), so the
MC equation places no constraint on~$\Omega$.  The specific
identification $\Omega = \sum \lambda_g$ requires showing that
the bar construction at genus~$g$ produces the class~$\lambda_g$
for multi-weight algebras, which is the content of
Open Problem~\ref{op:multi-generator-universality}.
% NOTE: The identification $\Omega_g = \lambda_g$ does not follow from
% $\dim H^2_{\mathrm{cyc}} = 1$ alone; it requires
% Open Problem~\ref{op:multi-generator-universality}.
\end{proof}

\begin{computation}[Explicit genus-$1$ value of the $W_3$ obstruction; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{comp:w3-obs-explicit}
For the $W_3$ minimal model $(p,q) = (5,4)$ with $c = \frac{4}{5}$,
the genus-$1$ curvature is
$\kappa(\mathcal{W}_3) = \frac{5c}{6} = \frac{2}{3}$
(Theorem~\ref{thm:w3-genus1-curvature}).  The genus-$1$ free energy is
\begin{equation}
F_1 \;=\; \frac{\kappa}{24}
\;=\; \frac{1}{36}.
\end{equation}
Both the $T$-channel ($T_{(3)}T = c/2 = 2/5$) and $W$-channel
($W_{(5)}W = c/3 = 4/15$) contribute through the \emph{same}
Hodge class $\lambda_1 = c_1(\mathbb{E})$, because the propagator
$K^{(1)}(z,w|\tau) = \partial_z\log\theta_1(z{-}w|\tau)$ is weight~$1$.
The Mumford isomorphism $c_1(R^0\pi_*\omega^{\otimes j}) = (6j^2{-}6j{+}1)\lambda_1$
is irrelevant: the higher Hodge bundles $\mathbb{E}_j$ for $j \geq 2$ do not appear in the
bar complex (Remark~\ref{rem:propagator-weight-universality}).
\end{computation}

\subsection{Verification: obstruction squares to zero}
\label{subsec:obstruction-squares-zero}

\begin{theorem}[Nilpotence of obstruction (\texorpdfstring{$g \leq 2$}{g <= 2}); \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:obstruction-nilpotent}
For any augmented chiral algebra $\mathcal{A}$ and genus $g \leq 2$, the genus-$g$ obstruction satisfies:
\begin{equation}
(\text{obs}_g)^2 = 0 \quad \text{in } H^4(\bar{B}_g(\mathcal{A}), Z(\mathcal{A}))
\end{equation}

This is a consistency condition ensuring the curved $A_\infty$ structure is well-defined.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
For $g \leq 2$, nilpotence follows from dimensional vanishing.
By Theorem~\ref{thm:genus-universality}, $\operatorname{obs}_g$ is
proportional to the Hodge class $\lambda_g$, which lies in
$H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, \mathbb{Q})$
(Table~\ref{tab:obstruction-summary}).  The cup product
$(\operatorname{obs}_g)^2$ therefore lies in
$H^{4g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, Z(\mathcal{A})^{\otimes 2})$.
Since $\dim_{\mathbb{R}} \overline{\mathcal{M}}_g = 6g - 6$, this
cohomology group vanishes whenever $4g > 6g - 6$, i.e., when
$g < 3$.
Case by case:
$g = 0$: $\operatorname{obs}_0 = 0$ by convention (the genus-$0$ bar complex has no curvature), so $(\operatorname{obs}_0)^2 = 0$;
$g = 1$: $(\operatorname{obs}_1)^2 \in H^4(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_1)$,
but $\dim_{\mathbb{R}} \overline{\mathcal{M}}_1 = 2$, so $H^4 = 0$;
$g = 2$: $(\operatorname{obs}_2)^2 \in H^8(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_2)$,
but $\dim_{\mathbb{R}} \overline{\mathcal{M}}_2 = 6$, so $H^8 = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Nilpotence of obstruction (all genera); \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{thm:obstruction-nilpotent-all-genera}
\label{thm:obstruction-nilpotent-higher}% backward-compatible label
\index{obstruction class!nilpotence!all genera}
For any Koszul chiral algebra $\mathcal{A}$ \emph{with a single strong
generator} and \emph{any} genus $g \geq 0$,
the genus-$g$ obstruction satisfies:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:obs-squared-zero-all-genera}
(\operatorname{obs}_g)^2 = 0 \quad \text{in }
H^{4g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g, Z(\mathcal{A})^{\otimes 2}).
\end{equation}
For multi-generator algebras, see
Remark~\textup{\ref{rem:multi-generator-nilpotence}} below.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
By Theorem~\ref{thm:genus-universality} (single strong generator hypothesis),
$\operatorname{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$ where
$\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E}) \in \mathrm{CH}^g(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$
is the top Chern class of the rank-$g$ Hodge bundle~$\mathbb{E}$.
Therefore $(\operatorname{obs}_g)^2 = \kappa^2 \cdot \lambda_g^2$,
and it suffices to show $\lambda_g^2 = 0$ in
$\mathrm{CH}^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$.

This is an immediate consequence of
\emph{Mumford's relation}~\cite{Mumford83}:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:mumford-relation}
c(\mathbb{E}) \cdot c(\mathbb{E}^\vee) = 1
\qquad \text{in } \mathrm{CH}^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g).
\end{equation}
Since $c_j(\mathbb{E}^\vee) = (-1)^j \lambda_j$ and
$\lambda_k = 0$ for $k > g$ (the Hodge bundle has rank~$g$),
extracting the degree-$2g$ component
of~\eqref{eq:mumford-relation} gives:
\[
\sum_{\substack{i + j = 2g \\ 0 \leq i,j \leq g}}
(-1)^j \lambda_i \lambda_j = 0.
\]
The only pair $(i,j)$ with $i + j = 2g$ and $0 \leq i,j \leq g$
is $i = j = g$.  The sum reduces to a single term:
\[
(-1)^g \lambda_g^2 = 0,
\]
whence $\lambda_g^2 = 0$ in
$\mathrm{CH}^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$ for all $g \geq 1$.

\emph{Mumford's relation.}  The Gauss--Manin connection on the
de~Rham cohomology sheaf
\[
\mathcal{H}^1_{\mathrm{dR}}
= R^1\pi_*\Omega^{\bullet}_{\mathcal{C}/\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g}(\log D)
\]
is flat, so
$c(\mathcal{H}^1_{\mathrm{dR}}) = 1$.
The Hodge filtration gives an exact sequence
$0 \to \mathbb{E} \to \mathcal{H}^1_{\mathrm{dR}}
\to \mathbb{E}^\vee \to 0$, whence
$c(\mathbb{E}) \cdot c(\mathbb{E}^\vee)
= c(\mathcal{H}^1_{\mathrm{dR}}) = 1$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Comparison with the dimensional argument]
\label{rem:mumford-vs-dimension}
For $g \leq 2$, Theorem~\textup{\ref{thm:obstruction-nilpotent}}
established $\lambda_g^2 = 0$ by the weaker observation that
$\mathrm{CH}^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g) = 0$ when
$2g > 3g - 3$, i.e., $g < 3$.
Mumford's relation is strictly stronger: it proves
$\lambda_g^2 = 0$ for \emph{all} genera, including $g = 3$
(where $\lambda_3^2 \in \mathrm{CH}^6(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_3)$
with $\dim \overline{\mathcal{M}}_3 = 6$, so the dimensional
argument fails) and all $g \geq 4$.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Multi-generator nilpotence]\label{rem:multi-generator-nilpotence}
\index{obstruction class!nilpotence!multi-generator}
For multi-generator algebras,
$\mathrm{obs}_1 = \kappa \cdot \lambda_1$
(Proposition~\ref{prop:multi-generator-obstruction}\textup{(a)}),
so $(\mathrm{obs}_1)^2 = \kappa^2 \cdot \lambda_1^2 = 0$
by Mumford's relation $\lambda_g^2 = 0$.
For uniform-weight algebras, the same nilpotence holds at all genera;
for multi-weight algebras at $g \geq 2$, nilpotence follows from
Theorem~\ref{thm:multi-generator-universality}.
\end{remark}

\begin{corollary}[Mumford multiplicative relations for obstruction classes; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]
\label{cor:mumford-multiplicative}
\index{Mumford relation!multiplicative consequences}
\index{obstruction class!multiplicative relations}
\index{lambda class@$\lambda$-class!product identities}
Let $\mathcal{A}$ be a Koszul chiral algebra with a single strong generator.
The obstruction classes
$\operatorname{obs}_g = \kappa(\mathcal{A}) \cdot \lambda_g$ satisfy the
following \emph{multiplicative relations}, inherited from
Mumford's relation~\eqref{eq:mumford-relation}:
\begin{enumerate}[label=\textup{(\alph*)}]
\item \textbf{Nilpotence} (Theorem~\ref{thm:obstruction-nilpotent-all-genera}):
$(\operatorname{obs}_g)^2 = 0$ for all $g \geq 1$.
\item \textbf{Degree-$k$ Mumford relations}: For each
$k = 1, \ldots, 2g$,
\begin{equation}\label{eq:mumford-degree-k}
\sum_{\substack{i + j = k \\ 0 \leq i,j \leq g}}
(-1)^j \operatorname{obs}_i \cdot \operatorname{obs}_j = 0
\qquad \text{in } \mathrm{CH}^k(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g) \otimes Z(\mathcal{A})^{\otimes 2}.
\end{equation}
\item \textbf{Recursive structure}: Setting $k = 2$ in~\eqref{eq:mumford-degree-k}
gives $(\operatorname{obs}_1)^2 = 2 \operatorname{obs}_2$. More generally, for $g \geq 3$,
setting $k = 2g - 2$ gives the recurrence:
\begin{equation}\label{eq:lambda-recurrence}
\lambda_{g-1}^2 = 2\lambda_g \cdot \lambda_{g-2}
\qquad \text{in } \mathrm{CH}^{2g-2}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g).
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Since $\operatorname{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$ by
Theorem~\ref{thm:genus-universality}, part~(b) follows from extracting
the degree-$k$ component of Mumford's relation
$c(\mathbb{E}) \cdot c(\mathbb{E}^\vee) = 1$, then scaling by
$\kappa^2$.  Part~(a) is the case $k = 2g$.

For part~(c), the degree-$2$ relation reads
$\lambda_2 - \lambda_1^2 + \lambda_2 = 0$, i.e.,
$\lambda_1^2 = 2\lambda_2$.  For the degree-$(2g-2)$ relation with
$g \geq 3$, the constraint $0 \leq i,j \leq g$ with $i + j = 2g - 2$
admits three pairs: $(g, g-2)$, $(g-1, g-1)$, $(g-2, g)$.
The relation becomes
\[
(-1)^{g-2}\lambda_g \lambda_{g-2}
+ (-1)^{g-1}\lambda_{g-1}^2
+ (-1)^{g}\lambda_{g-2}\lambda_g = 0.
\]
Since $\lambda_i \lambda_j = \lambda_j \lambda_i$ in
$\mathrm{CH}^*(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)$, factoring
$(-1)^{g-2}$ yields
$2\lambda_g \lambda_{g-2} - \lambda_{g-1}^2 = 0$.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Chern character reformulation]
\label{rem:chern-character-mumford}
Mumford's relation $c(\mathbb{E}) \cdot c(\mathbb{E}^\vee) = 1$
is equivalent to $\operatorname{ch}(\mathbb{E} \oplus \mathbb{E}^\vee)$ being
concentrated in degree~$0$, since $\mathbb{E} \oplus \mathbb{E}^\vee
= \mathcal{H}^1_{\mathrm{dR}}$ has trivial Chern character.
Via the Newton identities relating power sums to elementary symmetric
functions, all $\lambda$-class products are determined by the
Bernoulli-number weighted traces
$\operatorname{ch}_k(\mathbb{E}) = B_k \cdot \kappa_k / k!$
(where $\kappa_k$ are the Miller--Morita--Mumford classes),
giving the full multiplicative structure of the obstruction ring.
\end{remark}

\begin{example}[Heisenberg case]\label{ex:heisenberg-obs-squares}
For the Heisenberg algebra with
$\operatorname{obs}_g = \kappa \cdot \lambda_g$,
Theorem~\ref{thm:obstruction-nilpotent-all-genera} gives
$(\operatorname{obs}_g)^2 = \kappa^2 \cdot \lambda_g^2 = 0$
for \emph{all} $g \geq 1$, by Mumford's relation.
\end{example}

\subsection{Summary table: obstruction classes for key examples}
\label{subsec:obstruction-summary-table}

\begin{table}[ht]
\centering
\caption{Genus-$g$ obstruction classes}
\label{tab:obstruction-summary}
\begin{tabular}{|l|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Chiral Algebra} & \textbf{Obstruction $\text{obs}_g$} & \textbf{Physical Meaning} \\
\hline
Heisenberg $\mathcal{H}_\kappa$ & $\kappa \cdot \lambda_g$ & Level shift / central charge \\
\hline
$\widehat{\mathfrak{sl}}_2(k)$ & $\frac{3(k+2)}{4} \lambda_g$ & Affine level shift \\
\hline
$\widehat{\mathfrak{sl}}_3(k)$ & $\frac{4(k+3)}{3} \lambda_g$ & Affine level shift \\
\hline
$\widehat{E_8}(k)$ & $\frac{62(k+30)}{15} \lambda_g$ & Affine level shift \\
\hline
$W_3(c)$ & $\frac{5c}{6} \lambda_g$ & Conformal anomaly \\
\hline
Virasoro $(c)$ & $\frac{c}{2} \lambda_g$ & Conformal anomaly \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{theorem}[Genus universality; \ClaimStatusProvedHere]\label{thm:genus-universality}
\index{genus universality theorem|textbf}
\textup{[Regime: curved-central on the Koszul locus; all genera
\textup{(}Convention~\textup{\ref{conv:regime-tags})}.]}

Let $\cA$ be a modular Koszul chiral algebra whose strong generators
all have the \emph{same} conformal weight.  There exists a
genus-independent scalar $\kappa(\cA)$, determined by the genus-$1$
curvature, such that
\begin{equation}\label{eq:genus-universality}
\mathrm{obs}_g(\cA) \;=\; \kappa(\cA) \cdot \lambda_g
\qquad \text{in } H^{2g}(\overline{\mathcal{M}}_g)
\quad \text{for all } g \geq 1,
\end{equation}
where $\lambda_g = c_g(\mathbb{E})$ is the top Chern class of the
Hodge bundle $\mathbb{E} = R^0\pi_*\omega$.
For algebras with generators of \emph{several} conformal weights,
the genus-$1$ specialization is unconditional; the higher-genus
extension is conditional on the strong scalar ansatz of
Theorem~\ref{thm:multi-generator-universality}.

\begin{enumerate}
\item[\textup{(i)}] \emph{Explicit values.}
$\kappa(\mathcal{H}_\kappa) = \kappa$,\;
$\kappa(\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k) = \frac{(k+h^\vee)\dim\mathfrak{g}}{2h^\vee}$,\;
$\kappa(\mathrm{Vir}_c) = \frac{c}{2}$.
\item[\textup{(ii)}] \emph{Duality.}
For affine Kac--Moody algebras $\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_k$ with Feigin--Frenkel dual
$\widehat{\mathfrak{g}}_{-k-2h^\vee}$: $\kappa(\mathcal{A}^!) = -\kappa(\mathcal{A})$.
For $\mathcal{W}$-algebras $\mathcal{W}^k(\mathfrak{g}, f_{\mathrm{prin}})$ obtained by Drinfeld--Sokolov
reduction: $\kappa(\mathcal{A}^!) = \varrho(\mathfrak{g})\, K - \kappa(\mathcal{A})$, where
$K = c(\mathcal{A}) + c(\mathcal{A}^!)$ is the complementarity constant
(Remark~\ref{rem:vir-vs-km-complementarity}) and
$\varrho(\mathfrak{g}) = \sum_{i=1}^r \frac{1}{m_i + 1}$ is the exponent sum
($m_1, \ldots, m_r$ the exponents of~$\mathfrak{g}$; for $\mathcal{W}_N = \mathcal{W}(\mathfrak{sl}_N)$,
$\varrho = H_N - 1 = \sum_{s=2}^{N} 1/s$).
In particular, $\kappa + \kappa' = 0$ for Kac--Moody,
$\kappa + \kappa' = 13$ for the Virasoro algebra ($\varrho = 1/2$, $K = 26$),
and $\kappa + \kappa' = 250/3$ for $\mathcal{W}_3$ ($\varrho = 5/6$, $K = 100$).
The obstruction coefficient $\kappa = 0$ if and only if $\mathcal{A}$ is at critical level;
for Kac--Moody the dual is then also critical, but for $\mathcal{W}$-algebras
$\kappa(\mathcal{A}^!) = \varrho(\mathfrak{g})\, K \neq 0$.

\emph{Abelian case.}
